<td style="width:50%;border-right:1px dotted #aaa;">
<p><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka"><img alt="Sri Lanka" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1200" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Flag_of_Sri_Lanka.svg/23px-Flag_of_Sri_Lanka.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Flag_of_Sri_Lanka.svg/35px-Flag_of_Sri_Lanka.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Flag_of_Sri_Lanka.svg/46px-Flag_of_Sri_Lanka.svg.png 2x" width="23"/></a></span> <b><a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka_Armed_Forces" title="Sri Lanka Armed Forces">Sri Lanka Armed Forces</a></b>:<br/>
95,000 (2001)<br/>
118,000 (2002)<br/>
158,000 (2003)<br/>
151,000 (2004)<br/>
111,000 (2005)<br/>
150,900 (2006)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iiss1_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-iiss1-2">[2]</a></sup></p>

100,000 (peak)</td>

7,000 (2003)<br/>

</div>
<p>The <b>Sri Lankan Civil War</b> was an armed conflict fought on the island of <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>. Beginning on 23 July 1983, there was an intermittent <a href="/wiki/Insurgency" title="Insurgency">insurgency</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Sri_Lanka" title="Government of Sri Lanka">government</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Liberation_Tigers_of_Tamil_Eelam" title="Liberation Tigers of Tamil Eelam">Liberation Tigers of Tamil Eelam</a> (the LTTE, also known as the Tamil Tigers), which fought to create an <a href="/wiki/Independence" title="Independence">independent</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tamil_people" title="Tamil people">Tamil</a> state called <a href="/wiki/Tamil_Eelam" title="Tamil Eelam">Tamil Eelam</a> in the north and the east of the island. After a 26-year military campaign, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2008%E2%80%932009_Sri_Lankan_Army_Northern_offensive" title="2008–2009 Sri Lankan Army Northern offensive">Sri Lankan military defeated the Tamil Tigers in May 2009</a>, bringing the civil war to an end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mod-defeat_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-mod-defeat-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>For over 25 years, the war caused significant hardships for the population, environment and the <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Sri_Lanka" title="Economy of Sri Lanka">economy</a> of the country, with an initial estimated 80,000–100,000 people killed during its course.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ABC200509_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-ABC200509-14">[14]</a></sup> In 2013, the UN panel estimated additional deaths during the last phase of the war: "Around 40,000 died while other independent reports estimated the number of civilians dead to exceed 100,000."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> During the early part of the conflict, the Sri Lankan forces attempted to retake the areas captured by the LTTE. The tactics employed by the Liberation Tigers of Tamil Eelam against the actions of Government forces resulted in their listing as a terrorist organisation in 32 countries, including the United States, India, Canada and the member nations of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> The Sri Lankan government forces have also been accused of human rights abuses, systematic impunity for serious human rights violations, lack of respect for <a href="/wiki/Habeas_corpus" title="Habeas corpus">habeas corpus</a> in arbitrary detentions, and forced disappearances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>After two decades of fighting and four failed tries at peace talks, including the unsuccessful <a href="/wiki/Indian_intervention_in_the_Sri_Lankan_Civil_War" title="Indian intervention in the Sri Lankan Civil War">deployment of the Indian Army</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Peace_Keeping_Force" title="Indian Peace Keeping Force">Indian Peace Keeping Force</a> from 1987 to 1990, a lasting negotiated settlement to the conflict appeared possible when a cease-fire was declared in December 2001, and a ceasefire agreement signed with international mediation in 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gd1_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-gd1-21">[21]</a></sup> However, limited hostilities renewed in late 2005 and the conflict began to escalate until the government launched a number of major military offensives against the LTTE beginning in July 2006, driving the LTTE out of the entire <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Province,_Sri_Lanka" title="Eastern Province, Sri Lanka">Eastern province</a> of the island. The LTTE then declared they would "resume their freedom struggle to achieve statehood".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wt-7.2F14.2F07-ffo_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-wt-7.2F14.2F07-ffo-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt3_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt3-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2007, the government shifted its offensive to the north of the country, and formally announced its withdrawal from the ceasefire agreement on 2 January 2008, alleging that the LTTE violated the agreement over 10,000 times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mod-cfa-1.2F2.2F08_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-mod-cfa-1.2F2.2F08-24">[24]</a></sup> Since then, aided by the destruction of a number of large arms smuggling vessels that belonged to the LTTE,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mod-kkd-10.2F08.2F07_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-mod-kkd-10.2F08.2F07-25">[25]</a></sup> and an international crackdown on the funding for the Tamil Tigers, the government took control of the entire area previously controlled by the Tamil Tigers, including their de facto capital <a href="/wiki/Kilinochchi" title="Kilinochchi">Kilinochchi</a>, main military base <a href="/wiki/Mullaitivu" title="Mullaitivu">Mullaitivu</a> and the entire <a href="/wiki/A9_highway_(Sri_Lanka)" title="A9 highway (Sri Lanka)">A9 highway</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blm-1.2F26-rebelbase_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-blm-1.2F26-rebelbase-26">[26]</a></sup> leading the LTTE to finally admit defeat on 17 May 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-correspondentscivil_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-correspondentscivil-27">[27]</a></sup> Following the end of the war, the Sri Lankan government claimed Sri Lanka as the first country in the modern world to eradicate terrorism on its own soil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Following the LTTE's defeat, pro-LTTE <a href="/wiki/Tamil_National_Alliance" title="Tamil National Alliance">Tamil National Alliance</a> dropped its demand for a <a href="/wiki/Tamil_Eelam" title="Tamil Eelam">separate state</a>, in favour of a <a href="/wiki/Federalism" title="Federalism">federal</a> solution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc1_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc1-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardn_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-guardn-30">[30]</a></sup> In May 2010, <a href="/wiki/Mahinda_Rajapaksa" title="Mahinda Rajapaksa">Mahinda Rajapaksa</a>, the president of Sri Lanka, appointed the <a href="/wiki/Lessons_Learnt_and_Reconciliation_Commission" title="Lessons Learnt and Reconciliation Commission">Lessons Learnt and Reconciliation Commission</a> (LLRC) to assess the conflict between the time of the ceasefire agreement in 2002 and the defeat of the LTTE in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Resumption_of_hostilities"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Resumption of hostilities</span></a>
<ul>
<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Talks_and_further_violence"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Talks and further violence</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Mavil_Aru_water_dispute"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Mavil Aru water dispute</span></a></li>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_and_evolution">Origin and evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sri_Lankan_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Origin and evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Origins_of_the_Sri_Lankan_civil_war" title="Origins of the Sri Lankan civil war">Origins of the Sri Lankan civil war</a></div>
<p>The origins of the Sri Lankan Civil War lie in the continuous political rancor between the majority Sinhalese and the minority Tamils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The roots of the modern conflict lie in the <a href="/wiki/British_Ceylon" title="British Ceylon">British colonial rule</a> when the country was known as Ceylon. There was initially little tension among Sri Lanka's two largest ethnic groups, the <a href="/wiki/Sinhalese_people" title="Sinhalese people">Sinhalese</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sri_Lankan_Tamil_people" title="Sri Lankan Tamil people">Tamils</a>, when <a href="/wiki/Ponnambalam_Arunachalam" title="Ponnambalam Arunachalam">Ponnambalam Arunachalam</a>, a Tamil, was appointed representative of the Sinhalese as well the Tamils in the national legislative council. In 1919 major Sinhalese and Tamil political organizations united to form the Ceylon National Congress, under the leadership of Arunachalam, to press the colonial government for more constitutional reforms. However, British Gov. <a href="/wiki/William_Manning_(colonial_governor)" title="William Manning (colonial governor)">William Manning</a> actively encouraged the concept of "communal representation" and created the <a href="/wiki/Colombo" title="Colombo">Colombo</a> town seat in 1920, which dangled between the Tamils and the Sinhalese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tamllib_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-tamllib-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>After their election to the <a href="/wiki/State_Council_of_Ceylon" title="State Council of Ceylon">State Council</a> in 1936, the <a href="/wiki/Lanka_Sama_Samaja_Party" title="Lanka Sama Samaja Party">Lanka Sama Samaja Party</a> (LSSP) members <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N.M._Perera" title="N.M. Perera">N.M. Perera</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philip_Gunawardena" title="Philip Gunawardena">Philip Gunawardena</a> demanded the replacement of English as the official language by Sinhala and Tamil. In November 1936 a motion that "in the Municipal and Police Courts of the Island the proceedings should be in the <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">vernacular</a>" and that "entries in <a href="/wiki/Police_station" title="Police station">police stations</a> should be recorded in the language in which they are originally stated" were passed by the State Council and referred to the Legal Secretary. However, in 1944 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J.R._Jayawardene" title="J.R. Jayawardene">J.R. Jayawardene</a> moved in the State Council that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinhala_language" title="Sinhala language">Sinhala</a> should replace English as the official language.</p>
<p>In 1948, immediately after independence, a controversial law was passed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ceylon_Parliament" title="Ceylon Parliament">Ceylon Parliament</a> called the <a href="/wiki/Ceylon_Citizenship_Act" title="Ceylon Citizenship Act">Ceylon Citizenship Act</a>, which deliberately discriminated against the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Tamils_of_Sri_Lanka" title="Indian Tamils of Sri Lanka">Indian Tamil</a> ethnic minority by making it virtually impossible for them to obtain citizenship in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Approximately over 700,000 <a href="/wiki/Indian_Tamils_of_Sri_Lanka" title="Indian Tamils of Sri Lanka">Indian Tamils</a> were made stateless. Over the next three decades more than 300,000 <a href="/wiki/Indian_Tamils_of_Sri_Lanka" title="Indian Tamils of Sri Lanka">Indian Tamils</a> were deported back to India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> It wasn't until 2003–55 years after independence—that all <a href="/wiki/Indian_Tamils_of_Sri_Lanka" title="Indian Tamils of Sri Lanka">Indian Tamils</a> living in Sri Lanka were granted citizenship, but by this time they only made up 5% of the island's population.</p>
<p>In 1956 Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/S._W._R._D._Bandaranaike" title="S. W. R. D. Bandaranaike">S. W. R. D. Bandaranaike</a> passed the "<a href="/wiki/Sinhala_Only_Act" title="Sinhala Only Act">Sinhala Only Act</a>", which replaced <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinhala_language" title="Sinhala language">Sinhala</a> as the only <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a> of the country. This was seen as a deliberate attempt to discourage the <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lankan_Tamils" title="Sri Lankan Tamils">Sri Lankan Tamils</a> from working in the <a href="/wiki/Ceylon_Civil_Service" title="Ceylon Civil Service">Ceylon Civil Service</a> and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_services" title="Public services">public services</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Tamil_language" title="Tamil language">Tamil</a>-speaking minorities of Ceylon (Sri Lankan Tamils, <a href="/wiki/Indian_Tamils_of_Sri_Lanka" title="Indian Tamils of Sri Lanka">Indian Tamils</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lankan_Moors" title="Sri Lankan Moors">Sri Lankan Moors</a>) viewed the Act as linguistic, cultural and <a href="/wiki/Economic_discrimination" title="Economic discrimination">economic discrimination</a> against them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RP_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-RP-36">[36]</a></sup> Many Tamil-speaking <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_servants" title="Civil servants">civil servants</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_servants" title="Public servants">public servants</a> were forced to resign because they weren't <a href="/wiki/Fluency" title="Fluency">fluent</a> in Sinhala.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> This was a prelude to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gal_Oya_riots" title="Gal Oya riots">1956 Gal Oya riots</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1958_riots_in_Ceylon" title="1958 riots in Ceylon">1958 widespread riots</a> in which thousands of Tamil civilians perished. The civil war was a direct result of the escalation of the confrontational politics that followed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the late 1960s documents relating to a separate Tamil state of "<a href="/wiki/Tamil_Eelam" title="Tamil Eelam">Tamil Eelam</a>" began to circulate. At this time <a href="/wiki/Anton_Balasingham" title="Anton Balasingham">Anton Balasingham</a>, an employee of the British <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_Commission" title="High Commission">High Commission</a> in Colombo, began to participate in separatist activities. He later migrated to Britain, where he would become the chief theoretician of the LTTE. In the late 1960s several Tamil youth, among them <a href="/wiki/Velupillai_Prabhakaran" title="Velupillai Prabhakaran">Velupillai Prabhakaran</a>, also became involved in these activities. They carried out several hit-and-run operations against pro-government Tamil politicians, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka_police" title="Sri Lanka police">Sri Lanka police</a> and the civil administration.</p>
<p>During the 1970s<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Policy_of_standardization" title="Policy of standardization">Policy of standardization</a> was initiated. Under the policy, university selection was calculated based on language. Numbers of allocations were proportional to the number of applicants who sat in the examination in that language. Under this policy, university admissions were standardized to correct the disproportionately higher number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sri_Lankan_Tamil" title="Sri Lankan Tamil">Sri Lankan Tamil</a> students entering universities. Officially the policy was meant to discriminate in "favor" of students from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rural" title="Rural">rural</a> areas, but in reality the policy discriminated "against" Sri Lankan Tamil students who were in effect required to gain higher marks than Sinhalese students to gain admission to universities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RP_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-RP-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NI_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-NI-40">[40]</a></sup> For instance, the qualifying mark for admission to the medical faculties was 250 (out of 400) for Tamil students, whereas it was only 229 for Sinhalese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The number of Sri Lankan Tamil students entering universities fell dramatically. The policy was abandoned in 1977.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SriModernAge_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-SriModernAge-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other forms of official discrimination against the Sri Lankan Tamils included the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sri_Lankan_state_sponsored_colonisation_schemes" title="Sri Lankan state sponsored colonisation schemes">state-sponsored colonization</a> of traditional Tamil areas by Sinhalese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peasants" title="Peasants">peasants</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banning_of_Tamil-language_media_importation" title="Banning of Tamil-language media importation">banning</a> of the import of Tamil-language media and the preference given by the 1978 <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Sri_Lanka" title="Constitution of Sri Lanka">Constitution of Sri Lanka</a> to <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Sri_Lanka" title="Buddhism in Sri Lanka">Buddhism</a>, the main religion followed by the Sinhalese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RP_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-RP-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NI_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-NI-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>Prabhakaran—together with Chetti Thanabalasingam, a well known criminal from Kalviyankadu, Jaffna—formed the <a href="/wiki/Tamil_New_Tigers" title="Tamil New Tigers">Tamil New Tigers</a> (TNT) in 1972.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rohang_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-rohang-43">[43]</a></sup> This was formed around an ideology that looked back to the 1st Millennium <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chola_Empire" title="Chola Empire">Chola Empire</a>—the Tiger was the emblem of that empire.</p>
<p>A further movement, the <a href="/wiki/Eelam_Revolutionary_Organisation_of_Students" title="Eelam Revolutionary Organisation of Students">Eelam Revolutionary Organisation of Students</a> (EROS), formed in <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a> and London; it became the backbone of the Eelamist movement in the diaspora, arranging passports and employment for immigrants and levying a heavy tax on them. It became the basis of the Eelamist logistical organization, later taken over entirely by the LTTE. The formation of the <a href="/wiki/Tamil_United_Liberation_Front" title="Tamil United Liberation Front">Tamil United Liberation Front</a> (TULF) with the Vaddukkodei (Vattukottai) resolution of 1976 led to a hardening of attitudes. The resolution called for the creation of a secular, socialist state of Tamil Eelam, based on the right of <a href="/wiki/Self-determination" title="Self-determination">self-determination</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vaduk_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-vaduk-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>The TULF clandestinely supported the armed actions of the young militants who were dubbed <i>"our boys"</i>. TULF leader <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Appapillai_Amirthalingam" title="Appapillai Amirthalingam">Appapillai Amirthalingam</a> even provided letters of reference to the LTTE and to other Tamil insurgent groups to raise funds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rohang_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-rohang-43">[43]</a></sup> Amirthalingam introduced Prabhakaran to N.S. Krishnan, who later became the first international representative of LTTE. It was Krishnan who introduced Prabhakaran to Anton Balasingham, who later became the chief political strategist and chief negotiator of LTTE. The "boys" were the product of the post-war population explosion. Many partially educated, unemployed Tamil youth fell for revolutionary solutions to their problems. The leftist parties had remained "non-communal" for a long time, but the Federal Party (as well as its offshoot, the TULF), deeply conservative and dominated by <a href="/wiki/Vellalar" title="Vellalar">Vellalar</a> casteism, did not attempt to form a national alliance with the leftists in their fight for language rights.</p>
<p>Following the sweeping electoral victory of the <a href="/wiki/United_National_Party" title="United National Party">United National Party</a> (UNP) in July 1977, the TULF became the leading opposition party, with around one-sixth of the total electoral vote winning on a party platform of secession from Sri Lanka. After the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sri_Lankan_riots_of_1977" title="Sri Lankan riots of 1977">1977 riots</a> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Junius_Richard_Jayewardene" title="Junius Richard Jayewardene">J.R. Jayewardene</a> government made one concession to the Tamil population; it lifted the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Policy_of_standardization" title="Policy of standardization">policy of standardization</a> for university admission that had driven many Tamil youths into militancy. The concession was regarded by the militants as too little too late, and violent attacks continued. By this time TULF started losing its grip over the militant groups. LTTE ordered civilians to boycott the local government elections of 1983 in which even TULF contested. Voter turnout was as low as 10%. Thereafter, Tamil political parties were unable to represent the interests of the Tamil community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rohang_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-rohang-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Outbreak_of_civil_war">Outbreak of civil war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sri_Lankan_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Outbreak of civil war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Four_Four_Bravo" title="Four Four Bravo">Four Four Bravo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eelam_War_I" title="Eelam War I">Eelam War I</a></div>
<p>Supported by the ongoing politics of conflict in Sri Lanka, politicised Tamil youth in the north and east started to form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Tamil_militant_groups" title="List of Tamil militant groups">militant groups</a>. These groups developed independently of the Colombo Tamil leadership, and in the end rejected and annihilated them. The most prominent of these groups was the TNT, which changed its name to the Liberation Tigers of Tamil Eelam, or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LTTE" title="LTTE">LTTE</a>, in 1976. The LTTE initially carried out a campaign of violence against the state, particularly targeting policemen and also moderate Tamil politicians who attempted a dialogue with the government. Their first major operation was the assassination of the mayor of Jaffna, <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Duraiappah" title="Alfred Duraiappah">Alfred Duraiappah</a>, in 1975 by Prabhakaran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffman139_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffman139-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>In May 1981 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burning_of_the_Jaffna_library" title="Burning of the Jaffna library">burning of the Jaffna library</a> by politicians from the ruling party using police and paramilitary forces resulted in the destruction of more than 90,000 books, including "palm leaf scrolls" of immense historical value. This violent example of ethnic <a href="/wiki/Book_burning" title="Book burning">biblioclasm</a> was a major turning point in convincing the Tamil people that the government could not protect them or their cultural heritage and persuaded many of them to back a separate state.</p>
<p>The LTTE's <i>modus operandi</i> of the early war was based on assassinations, whereas the mode of operation for the UNP was through a series of checkpoints set up around the city. The assassination in 1977 of a Tamil Member of Parliament, M. Canagaratnam, was carried out personally by Prabhakaran, the leader of the LTTE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> In July 1983 the LTTE launched a deadly ambush on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka_army" title="Sri Lanka army">Sri Lanka army</a> patrol <a href="/wiki/Four_Four_Bravo" title="Four Four Bravo">Four Four Bravo</a> outside the town of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thirunelveli_(Sri_Lanka)" title="Thirunelveli (Sri Lanka)">Thirunelveli</a>, killing an officer and 12 soldiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Using nationalistic sentiments to their advantage, the Jayawardena organized massacres and <a href="/wiki/Pogrom" title="Pogrom">pogroms</a> in <a href="/wiki/Colombo" title="Colombo">Colombo</a><sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_.7B.7B.7B1.7D.7D.7D"><a class="external autonumber" href="//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sri_Lankan_Civil_War#endnote_">[1]</a></sup>, the capital, and elsewhere (see <a href="/wiki/Black_July" title="Black July">Black July</a>). Between 400-3,000 Tamils were estimated to have been killed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> and many more fled Sinhalese-majority areas. This is considered the beginning of the civil war.</p>
<p>Apart from the LTTE, there initially was a plethora of militant groups (see <a href="/wiki/List_of_Sri_Lankan_Tamil_militant_groups" title="List of Sri Lankan Tamil militant groups">list</a>). The LTTE's position, adopted from that of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palestinian_Liberation_Organization" title="Palestinian Liberation Organization">PLO</a>, was that there should be only one. Initially, the LTTE gained prominence due to devastating attacks such as the <a href="/wiki/Kent_and_Dollar_Farm_massacres" title="Kent and Dollar Farm massacres">Kent and Dollar Farm massacres</a> of 1984, where hundreds of men, women and children were attacked during the night as they slept and were hacked to death with fatal blows to the head from axes; and the <a href="/wiki/Anuradhapura_massacre" title="Anuradhapura massacre">Anuradhapura massacre</a> of 1985, where the LTTE indiscriminately opened fire, killing and wounding 146 civilians within <a href="/wiki/Jaya_Sri_Maha_Bodhi" title="Jaya Sri Maha Bodhi">Jaya Sri Maha Bodhi</a> Buddhist shrine. The Anuradhapura massacre was apparently answered by government forces with the <a href="/wiki/Kumudini_boat_massacre" title="Kumudini boat massacre">Kumudini boat massacre</a> in which over 23 Tamil civilians died. Over time the LTTE merged with or largely exterminated almost all the other militant Tamil groups. As a result, many Tamil splinter groups ended up working with the Sri Lankan government as paramilitaries or denounced violence and joined mainstream politics; some legitimate Tamil-oriented political parties remained, all opposed to LTTE's vision of an independent state.</p>
<p>Peace talks between the LTTE and the government began in <a href="/wiki/Thimphu" title="Thimphu">Thimphu</a> in 1985, but they soon failed and the war continued. In 1986 many civilians were <a href="/wiki/Akkaraipattu_massacre" title="Akkaraipattu massacre">massacred</a> as part of this conflict. In 1987 government troops pushed LTTE fighters to the northern city of <a href="/wiki/Jaffna" title="Jaffna">Jaffna</a>. In April 1987 the conflict exploded with ferocity, as both government forces and LTTE fighters engaged in a series of bloody operations.</p>
<p>The Sri Lankan military launched an offensive, called "Operation Liberation" or <a href="/wiki/Vadamarachchi_Operation" title="Vadamarachchi Operation">Vadamarachchi Operation</a>, during May–June 1987 to regain control of the territory in the Jaffna peninsula from the LTTE. This marked the Sri Lankan military's first conventional warfare on Sri Lankan soil since independence. The offensive was successful, and LTTE leader Prabhakaran and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_Tiger" title="Sea Tiger">Sea Tiger</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Thillaiyampalam_Sivanesan" title="Thillaiyampalam Sivanesan">Thillaiyampalam Sivanesan</a> <i>alias</i> Soosai narrowly escaped from advancing troops at Valvettithurai. Key military personnel involved in the operation were Lt Col. Vipul Boteju, Lt. Col. Sarath Jayawardane, Col. <a href="/wiki/Vijaya_Wimalaratne" title="Vijaya Wimalaratne">Vijaya Wimalaratne</a> and Brig. Gen. <a href="/wiki/Denzil_Kobbekaduwa" title="Denzil Kobbekaduwa">Denzil Kobbekaduwa</a>.</p>
<p>In July 1987 the LTTE carried out their first suicide attack. <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Capt._Miller&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Capt. Miller (page does not exist)">Capt. Miller</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Tigers" title="Black Tigers">Black Tigers</a> drove a small truck carrying explosives through the wall of a fortified Sri Lankan army camp, reportedly killing 40 soldiers. The LTTE out over 378 <a href="/wiki/Suicide_attack" title="Suicide attack">suicide attacks</a>, one of the largest suicide campaigns in the world, and the suicide attack became a trademark of the LTTE and a characteristic of the civil war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rep_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-rep-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pc_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-pc-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Indian_involvement">Indian involvement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sri_Lankan_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Indian involvement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="vertical-navbox vcard" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%">

<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Politics_of_Sri_Lanka_sidebar" title="Template:Politics of Sri Lanka sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Politics_of_Sri_Lanka_sidebar" title="Template talk:Politics of Sri Lanka sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Politics_of_Sri_Lanka_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>

<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Indian_intervention_in_the_Sri_Lankan_Civil_War" title="Indian intervention in the Sri Lankan Civil War">Indian intervention in the Sri Lankan Civil War</a> and <a href="/wiki/Research_and_Analysis_Wing" title="Research and Analysis Wing">Research and Analysis Wing</a></div>
<p>India became involved in the conflict in the 1980s for a number of reasons, including its leaders' desire to project India as the regional power in the area and worries about India's own Tamils seeking independence. Involvement was particularly strong in the Indian state of <a href="/wiki/Tamil_Nadu" title="Tamil Nadu">Tamil Nadu</a>, where ethnic kinship led to strong support for the independence of Sri Lankan Tamils. Throughout the conflict the Indian central and state governments supported both sides in different ways. From August 1983 until May 1987 the Indian government, through its intelligence agency <a href="/wiki/Research_and_Analysis_Wing" title="Research and Analysis Wing">Research and Analysis Wing</a> (RAW), provided arms, training and monetary support to six Sri Lankan Tamil militant groups including LTTE, <a href="/wiki/Tamil_Eelam_Liberation_Organization" title="Tamil Eelam Liberation Organization">Tamil Eelam Liberation Organization</a> (TELO), <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Liberation_Organisation_of_Tamil_Eelam" title="People's Liberation Organisation of Tamil Eelam">People's Liberation Organisation of Tamil Eelam</a> (PLOTE), <a href="/wiki/Eelam_Revolutionary_Organisation_of_Students" title="Eelam Revolutionary Organisation of Students">Eelam Revolutionary Organisation of Students</a> (EROS) <a href="/wiki/Eelam_People%27s_Revolutionary_Liberation_Front" title="Eelam People's Revolutionary Liberation Front">Eelam People's Revolutionary Liberation Front</a> (EPRLF) and <a href="/wiki/Tamil_Eelam_Liberation_Army" title="Tamil Eelam Liberation Army">Tamil Eelam Liberation Army</a> (TELA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> LTTE's rise is widely attributed to the initial backing it received from RAW. It is believed that by supporting different militant groups, the Indian government hoped to keep the Tamil independence movement divided and be able to exert overt control over it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-at1_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-at1-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>India became more actively involved in the late 1980s, and on 5 June 1987 the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Air_Force" title="Indian Air Force">Indian Air Force</a> <a href="/wiki/Operation_Poomalai" title="Operation Poomalai">airdropped food parcels</a> to <a href="/wiki/Jaffna" title="Jaffna">Jaffna</a> while it was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operation_Liberation" title="Operation Liberation">under siege</a> by Sri Lankan forces. At a time when the Sri Lankan government stated it was close to defeating the LTTE, India dropped 25 tons of food and medicine by parachute into areas held by the LTTE in a direct move of support to the rebels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Negotiations were held, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-Sri_Lanka_Peace_Accord" title="Indo-Sri Lanka Peace Accord">Indo-Sri Lanka Peace Accord</a> was signed on 29 July 1987 by Indian Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Rajiv_Gandhi" title="Rajiv Gandhi">Rajiv Gandhi</a> and Sri Lankan President <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Junius_Richard_Jayewardene" title="Junius Richard Jayewardene">Jayewardene</a>. Under this accord the Sri Lankan government made a number of concessions to Tamil demands, including <a href="/wiki/Devolution" title="Devolution">devolution</a> of power to the <a href="/wiki/Provinces_of_Sri_Lanka" title="Provinces of Sri Lanka">provinces</a>, a merger—subject to later referendum—of the northern and eastern provinces into a <a href="/wiki/North_Eastern_Province,_Sri_Lanka" title="North Eastern Province, Sri Lanka">single province</a>, and official status for the Tamil language (this was enacted as the 13th Amendment to the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Sri_Lanka" title="Constitution of Sri Lanka">Constitution of Sri Lanka</a>). India agreed to establish order in the north and east through the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Peace_Keeping_Force" title="Indian Peace Keeping Force">Indian Peace Keeping Force</a> (IPKF) and to cease assisting Tamil insurgents. Militant groups including the LTTE, although initially reluctant, agreed to surrender their arms to the IPKF, which initially oversaw a cease-fire and a modest <a href="/wiki/Disarmament" title="Disarmament">disarmament</a> of the militant groups.</p>
<p>The signing of the Indo-Lanka Accord, so soon after J.R. Jayawardene's declaration that he would fight the Indians to the last bullet, led to unrest in the south. The arrival of the IPKF to take control of most areas in the north of the country enabled the Sri Lanka government to shift its forces to the south (in Indian aircraft) to quell the protests. This led to an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insurrection_1987-89" title="Insurrection 1987-89">uprising</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Janatha_Vimukthi_Peramuna" title="Janatha Vimukthi Peramuna">Janatha Vimukthi Peramuna</a> in the south, which was put down bloodily over the next two years.</p>
<p>While most Tamil militant groups laid down their weapons and agreed to seek a peaceful solution to the conflict, the LTTE refused to disarm its fighters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cp1_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-cp1-53">[53]</a></sup> Keen to ensure the success of the accord, the IPKF then tried to demobilize the LTTE by force and ended up in full-scale conflict with them. The three-year-long conflict was also marked by the IPKF being accused of committing various abuses by many human rights groups as well as some within the Indian media. The IPKF also soon met stiff opposition from the Tamils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Simultaneously, nationalist sentiment led many Sinhalese to oppose the continued Indian presence in Sri Lanka. These led to the Sri Lankan government's call for India to quit the island, and the government allegedly entered into a secret deal with the LTTE that culminated in a cease-fire. However, the LTTE and IPKF continued to have frequent clashes. In April 1989 the <a href="/wiki/Ranasinghe_Premadasa" title="Ranasinghe Premadasa">Ranasinghe Premadasa</a> government ordered the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka_army" title="Sri Lanka army">Sri Lanka army</a> to clandestinely hand over arms consignments to the LTTE to fight the IPKF and its proxy Tamil National Army (TNA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atimesd_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-atimesd-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Although casualties among the IPKF mounted, and calls for the withdrawal of the IPKF from both sides of the Sri Lankan conflict grew, Gandhi refused to remove the IPKF from Sri Lanka. However, following her defeat in Indian parliamentary elections in December 1989, new Prime Minister <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/V.P._Singh" title="V.P. Singh">V.P. Singh</a> ordered the withdrawal of the IPKF, and their last ship left Sri Lanka on 24 March 1990. The 32-month presence of the IPKF in Sri Lanka resulted in the deaths of 1200 Indian soldiers and over 5000 Sri Lankans. The cost for the Indian government was estimated at over ₹10.3 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-para_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-para-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rajiv_Gandhi.27s_assassination">Rajiv Gandhi's assassination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sri_Lankan_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Rajiv Gandhi's assassination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Rajiv_Gandhi" title="Assassination of Rajiv Gandhi">Assassination of Rajiv Gandhi</a></div>
<p>Support for the LTTE in India dropped considerably in 1991, after the assassination of ex-Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Rajiv_Gandhi" title="Rajiv Gandhi">Rajiv Gandhi</a> by a female suicide bomber, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thenmozhi_Rajaratnam" title="Thenmozhi Rajaratnam">Thenmozhi Rajaratnam</a>. The Indian press subsequently reported that Prabhakaran decided to eliminate Gandhi, as he considered the ex-Prime Minister to be against the Tamil liberation struggle and feared that he might re-induct the IPKF, which Prabhakaran termed the "satanic force", if he won the <a href="/wiki/Indian_general_election,_1991" title="Indian general election, 1991">1991 Indian general election</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> In 1998 a court in India presided over by Special Judge V. Navaneetham found the LTTE and its leader Velupillai Prabhakaran responsible for the assassination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> In a 2006 interview, LTTE ideologue Anton Balasingham expressed regret over the assassination, although he stopped short of outright acceptance of responsibility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> India remained an outside observer of the conflict after the assassination.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Eelam_War_II_.281990.E2.80.931995.29">Eelam War II (1990–1995)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sri_Lankan_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Eelam War II (1990–1995)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elephant_Pass_armored_bulldozer.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1896" data-file-width="2720" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Elephant_Pass_armored_bulldozer.JPG/300px-Elephant_Pass_armored_bulldozer.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Elephant_Pass_armored_bulldozer.JPG/450px-Elephant_Pass_armored_bulldozer.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Elephant_Pass_armored_bulldozer.JPG/600px-Elephant_Pass_armored_bulldozer.JPG 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elephant_Pass_armored_bulldozer.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Improvised armored <a href="/wiki/Bulldozer" title="Bulldozer">bulldozer</a> used by the LTTE in the <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_Elephant_Pass" title="First Battle of Elephant Pass">First Battle of Elephant Pass</a> (1991), one of the major battles. This bulldozer was destroyed by Cpl. <a href="/wiki/Gamini_Kularatne" title="Gamini Kularatne">Gamini Kularatne</a>. Today it stands on display as a war memorial.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Eelam_War_II" title="Eelam War II">Eelam War II</a></div>
<p>The violence continued unabated despite the steps taken to appease Tamil sentiments, such as the <a href="/wiki/Thirteenth_Amendment_to_the_Constitution_of_Sri_Lanka" title="Thirteenth Amendment to the Constitution of Sri Lanka">13th Amendment</a> (enacted in November 1987). Meanwhile, the Chief Minister of the then <a href="/wiki/North_Eastern_Province,_Sri_Lanka" title="North Eastern Province, Sri Lanka">North and East Provincial Council</a>, <a href="/wiki/Annamalai_Varadaraja_Perumal" title="Annamalai Varadaraja Perumal">Vartharaja Perumal</a>, put forward a 19-point demand to resolve the ethnic crisis. He threatened if these demands were not met that the Provincial Council would go ahead with a <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_independence" title="Declaration of independence">unilateral declaration of independence</a> of the northern and eastern provinces, as in the case of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unilateral_Declaration_of_Independence" title="Unilateral Declaration of Independence">Rhodesia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> President Premadasa moved to quickly dissolve the Council (March 1990). At the same time LTTE used terror tactics to scare Sinhalese and Muslim farmers away from the north and east of the island, and swiftly took control of a significant portion of the territory. When the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Peace_Keeping_Force" title="Indian Peace Keeping Force">Indian Peace-Keeping Force</a> withdrew in 1989–90, the LTTE established many government-like functions in the areas under its control. A tentative cease-fire held in 1990 as the LTTE occupied itself with destroying rival Tamil groups while the government cracked down on the JVP uprising. When both major combatants had established their power bases, they turned on each other and the cease-fire broke down.The government launched an offensive to try to retake Jaffna.</p>
<p>This phase of the war soon acquired the name <i>Eelam War II</i>, and featured unprecedented brutality. On 11 June 1990 the LTTE <a href="/wiki/1990_massacre_of_Sri_Lankan_Police_officers" title="1990 massacre of Sri Lankan Police officers">massacred 600 policemen</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Province,_Sri_Lanka" title="Eastern Province, Sri Lanka">Eastern Province</a> after they had surrendered on promises of safe-conduct. The government placed an embargo on food and medicine entering the Jaffna peninsula and the air force relentlessly bombed LTTE targets in the area. The LTTE responded by <a href="/wiki/List_of_attacks_attributed_to_the_LTTE#1990" title="List of attacks attributed to the LTTE">attacking Sinhalese and Muslim villages</a> and massacring civilians. One of the largest civilian massacres of the war occurred when the LTTE <a href="/wiki/Palliyagodella_massacre" title="Palliyagodella massacre">massacred 166 Muslim civilians at Palliyagodella</a>. The government trained and armed Home Guard Muslim units, then took revenge on Tamil villages. There was also a significant massacre of Tamil civilians attributed<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (May 2015)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> to government forces, especially in the Eastern Province.</p>
<p>Notable international jurist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neelan_Thiruchelvam" title="Neelan Thiruchelvam">Neelan Thiruchelvam</a>, in a speech at the ICES-Colombo, indicated that the appropriate investigations into <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/massacre" title="wikt:massacre">massacres</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forced_disappearances" title="Forced disappearances">disappearances</a> of civilians including many children in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sathurukondan_massacre" title="Sathurukondan massacre">Sathurukondan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eastern_University_massacre" title="Eastern University massacre">Eastern University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mylanthanai_massacre" title="Mylanthanai massacre">Mylanthanai</a> and the mass murder and burial of school children at <a href="/wiki/Sooriyakanda_mass_grave" title="Sooriyakanda mass grave">Sooriyakanda</a> were hampered by the adoption of emergency regulations which contributed to a climate of impunity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Along roadsides in the north and east, burning bodies became a common sight. Throughout the country, government death squads hunted down, kidnapped or killed Sinhalese or Tamil youth suspected of sympathizing with the JVP or the LTTE, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> In October 1990 the LTTE <a href="/wiki/Expulsion_of_Muslims_from_the_Northern_province_by_LTTE" title="Expulsion of Muslims from the Northern province by LTTE">expelled all the Muslims residing in Northern province</a>. A total of 72,000 Muslims were forced to leave their homes, taking nothing but the clothes on their backs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>The largest battle of the war took place in July 1991, when 5,000 LTTE fighters surrounded the army's <a href="/wiki/Elephant_Pass" title="Elephant Pass">Elephant Pass</a> base, which controlled access to the <a href="/wiki/Jaffna_Peninsula" title="Jaffna Peninsula">Jaffna Peninsula</a>. More than 2,000 died on both sides in the month-long siege, before 10,000 government troops arrived to relieve the base.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> In February 1992 another series of government offensives failed to capture Jaffna. Lt. Gen. <a href="/wiki/Denzil_Kobbekaduwa" title="Denzil Kobbekaduwa">Denzil Kobbekaduwa</a> together with Maj. Gen. <a href="/wiki/Vijaya_Wimalaratne" title="Vijaya Wimalaratne">Vijaya Wimalaratne</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Rear_Adm.&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Rear Adm. (page does not exist)">Rear Adm.</a> Mohan Jayamaha, died on 8 August 1992 at Araly (Aeraella) point <a href="/wiki/Jaffna" title="Jaffna">Jaffna</a> due to a <a href="/wiki/Land_mine" title="Land mine">land mine</a> blast. Their deaths badly affected military morale. The LTTE, for its part, scored a major victory when one of its suicide bombers killed Sri Lankan President <a href="/wiki/Ranasinghe_Premadasa" title="Ranasinghe Premadasa">Ranasinghe Premadasa</a> in May 1993. In November 1993 the LTTE defeated the army in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Pooneryn" title="Battle of Pooneryn">Battle of Pooneryn</a>. This attack left 532 Sri Lankan soldiers and 135 sailors either dead or <a href="/wiki/Missing_in_action" title="Missing in action">missing in action</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rep_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-rep-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Eelam_War_III">Eelam War III</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sri_Lankan_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Eelam War III">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Eelam_War_III" title="Eelam War III">Eelam War III</a></div>
<p>In the 1994 parliamentary elections the UNP was defeated and, amidst great hope, the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Alliance_(Sri_Lanka)" title="People's Alliance (Sri Lanka)">People's Alliance</a>, headed by <a href="/wiki/Chandrika_Kumaratunga" title="Chandrika Kumaratunga">Chandrika Kumaratunga</a>, came to power on a peace platform. During the <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lankan_presidential_election,_1994" title="Sri Lankan presidential election, 1994">Presidential election campaign</a> an LTTE bomb attack was carried out during a rally held at Thotalanga, <a href="/wiki/Grandpass" title="Grandpass">Grandpass</a>, eliminated the entire top brass of UNP leadership, including its presidential candidate, <a href="/wiki/Gamini_Dissanayake" title="Gamini Dissanayake">Gamini Dissanayake</a>. Kumaratunga became the president with a 62% majority. A cease-fire was agreed to in January 1995, but the ensuing negotiations proved fruitless. The LTTE broke the cease-fire and blew up two <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka_Navy" title="Sri Lanka Navy">Sri Lanka Navy</a> gunboats known as SLNS "Sooraya" and "Ranasuru" on 19 April, thereby beginning the next phase of the war, dubbed <i>Eelam War III</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hrw2_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-hrw2-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>The new government then pursued a policy of "war for peace". Determined to retake the key rebel stronghold of Jaffna, which was occupied by 2,000 rebels,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt1_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt1-70">[70]</a></sup> it poured troops into the peninsula. In one particular incident in August 1995, Air Force jets bombed St. Peter's church at Navali (Naavaella), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Navaly_Church_massacre" title="Navaly Church massacre">killing</a> at least 65 refugees and wounding 150 others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> In another instance in the same year, over 40 people were massacred in <a href="/wiki/Nagerkovil_school_bombing" title="Nagerkovil school bombing">Nagerkovil</a> and more civilian massacres followed in subsequent years, such as the <a href="/wiki/Kumarapuram_massacre" title="Kumarapuram massacre">Kumarapuram massacre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tampalakamam_massacre" title="Tampalakamam massacre">Tampalakamam massacre</a>, <a href="/wiki/1999_Puthukkudiyiruppu_bombing" title="1999 Puthukkudiyiruppu bombing">Puthukkudiyiruppu massacre</a>, etc., all of them carried out by government forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amnesty_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-amnesty-72">[72]</a></sup> Government troops initially cut off the peninsula from the rest of the island,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt1_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt1-70">[70]</a></sup> and then, after seven weeks of heavy fighting, succeeded in bringing Jaffna under government control for the first time in nearly a decade. In a high-profile ceremony, Sri Lankan Defense Minister <a href="/wiki/Anuruddha_Ratwatte" title="Anuruddha Ratwatte">Anuruddha Ratwatte</a> raised the national flag inside the <a href="/wiki/Jaffna_Fort" title="Jaffna Fort">Jaffna Fort</a> on 5 December 1995. The government estimated that approximately 2500 soldiers and rebels were killed in the offensive, and an estimated 7,000 wounded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnn1_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnn1-73">[73]</a></sup> Many civilians were killed in this conflict, such as the <a href="/wiki/Navaly_church_bombing" title="Navaly church bombing">Navaly church bombing</a> in which over 125 civilians died. The LTTE and more than 350,000 civilians, compelled by LTTE pressure to leave Jaffna,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hrw1_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-hrw1-74">[74]</a></sup> fled to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vanni_region" title="Vanni region">Vanni region</a> in the interior. Most of the refugees returned later the next year.</p>
<p>The LTTE responded by launching <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operation_Unceasing_Waves_I" title="Operation Unceasing Waves I">Operation Unceasing Waves</a> and decisively won the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mullaitivu_(1996)" title="Battle of Mullaitivu (1996)">Battle of Mullaitivu</a> on 18 July 1996, leaving 1,173 army troops dead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rep_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-rep-4">[4]</a></sup> The government launched another offensive in August 1996. Another 200,000 civilians fled the violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hrw1_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-hrw1-74">[74]</a></sup> The town of <a href="/wiki/Kilinochchi" title="Kilinochchi">Kilinochchi</a> was taken on 29 September. On 13 May 1997, 20,000 government troops tried to open a supply line through the LTTE-controlled Vanni, but failed.</p>
<p>As violence continued in the North, LTTE suicide and time bombs were exploded numerous times in populated city areas and public transport in the south of the country, killing hundreds of civilians. In January 1996 the LTTE carried out one of its deadliest suicide bomb attacks at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_Bank_Bombing" title="Central Bank Bombing">Central Bank</a> in Colombo, killing 90 and injuring 1,400. In October 1997 it bombed the Sri Lankan World Trade Centre and, in January 1998, detonated a truck bomb in <a href="/wiki/Kandy" title="Kandy">Kandy</a>, damaging the <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_the_Tooth" title="Temple of the Tooth">Temple of the Tooth</a>, one of the holiest Buddhist shrines in the world. In response to this bombing, the Sri Lankan government outlawed the LTTE and with some success pressed other governments around the world to do the same, significantly interfering with the group's fund-raising activities.</p>
<p>In January 1997 heavy fighting around Paranthan and the Elephant Pass complex took the lives of 223 Army troops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rep_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-rep-4">[4]</a></sup> On 27 September 1998 the LTTE launched <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operation_Unceasing_Waves_II" title="Operation Unceasing Waves II">Operation Unceasing Waves II</a> and, after heavy fighting, captured Kilinochchi, winning the <a href="/wiki/1998_Battle_of_Kilinochchi" title="1998 Battle of Kilinochchi">Battle of Kilinochchi</a>. Clashes around the Kilinochchi forward defense line claimed the lives of 1206 soldiers that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rep_4-5"><a href="#cite_note-rep-4">[4]</a></sup> In March 1999, in Operation Rana Gosa, the government tried invading the Vanni from the south.The army made some gains, taking control of Oddusuddan (Oththan-thuduva) and Madhu, but could not dislodge the LTTE from the region. In September 1999 the LTTE <a href="/wiki/Gonagala_massacre" title="Gonagala massacre">massacred 50 Sinhalese civilians at Gonagala</a>.</p>
<p>The LTTE returned to the offensive with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operation_Unceasing_Waves_III" title="Operation Unceasing Waves III">Operation Unceasing Waves III</a> on 2 November 1999. Nearly all the Vanni rapidly fell back into LTTE hands. The group launched 17 successful attacks in the region, culminating in the overrunning of the Paranthan (Puranthaenna) Chemicals Factory base and the Kurrakkan Kaddukulam (kurakkan-kaela vaeva) base.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tamilnet_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-tamilnet-75">[75]</a></sup> The death toll amouned to 516 soldiers dead and over 4,000 injured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rep_4-6"><a href="#cite_note-rep-4">[4]</a></sup> The rebels also advanced north towards Elephant Pass and Jaffna. The LTTE was successful in cutting all land and sea supply lines of the Sri Lankan armed forces to the south, west and north of the town of Kilinochchi. In December 1999 the LTTE attempted to assassinate President Chandrika Kumaratunga in a suicide attack at a pre-election rally. She lost her right-eye, among other injuries, but was able to defeat opposition leader <a href="/wiki/Ranil_Wickremesinghe" title="Ranil Wickremesinghe">Ranil Wickremesinghe</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lankan_presidential_election,_1999" title="Sri Lankan presidential election, 1999">Presidential election</a> and was re-elected to her second term in office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tribune1_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-tribune1-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 22 April 2000 the Elephant Pass military complex, which had separated the Jaffna peninsula from the Vanni mainland for 17 years, fell into the hands of the LTTE, leaving 1,008 soldiers dead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rep_4-7"><a href="#cite_note-rep-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> The army then launched Operation Agni Kheela to take back the southern Jaffna Peninsula, but sustained losses. The LTTE continued to press towards Jaffna, and many feared it would fall to the LTTE, but the military repulsed LTTE attacks and was able to maintain control of the city.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_peace_efforts">Early peace efforts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sri_Lankan_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Early peace efforts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Exhaustion with the war was building as casualties mounted and there appeared to be no end in sight. By mid-2000 human rights groups estimated that more than one million people in Sri Lanka were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internally_displaced_persons" title="Internally displaced persons">internally displaced persons</a>, living in camps, homeless and struggling for survival. As a result, a significant <a href="/wiki/Peace_movement" title="Peace movement">peace movement</a> developed in the late 1990s, with many organizations holding peace camps, conferences, trainings and peace meditations, and many other efforts to bridge the two sides at all levels. As early as February 2000 Norway was asked to mediate by both sides, and initial international diplomatic moves began to find a negotiated settlement to the conflict.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hopes for peace gained ground as the LTTE declared a unilateral cease-fire in December 2000, but they cancelled it on 24 April 2001 and launched another offensive against the government. After securing a vast area kformerly controlled by the military, the LTTE further advanced northwards. This advancement posed a serious threat to the Elephant Pass military complex that housed 17,000 Sri Lankan troops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>In July 2001 the LTTE carried out a devastating <a href="/wiki/Bandaranaike_Airport_attack" title="Bandaranaike Airport attack">suicide attack on Bandaranaike International Airport</a>, destroying eight of the air force's planes (two <a href="/wiki/IAI_Kfir" title="IAI Kfir">IAI Kfirs</a>, one <a href="/wiki/Mil_Mi-17" title="Mil Mi-17">Mil-17</a>, one <a href="/wiki/Mil_Mi-24" title="Mil Mi-24">Mil-24</a>, three <a href="/wiki/Hongdu_JL-8" title="Hongdu JL-8">K-8 trainers</a>, one <a href="/wiki/Mikoyan_MiG-27" title="Mikoyan MiG-27">MiG-27</a>) and four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sri_Lankan_Airlines" title="Sri Lankan Airlines">Sri Lankan Airlines</a> planes (two <a href="/wiki/Airbus" title="Airbus">Airbus</a> <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A330" title="Airbus A330">A330s</a>, one <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A340" title="Airbus A340">A340</a> and one <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Airbus_A320" title="Airbus A320">A320</a>), dampening the economy and causing tourism—a vital foreign exchange earner for the government—to plummet. The impact of the attack was such that that year the <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Sri_Lanka" title="Economy of Sri Lanka">Sri Lankan economy</a> recorded a negative growth for the first and only time since its independence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="2002_Peace_Process">2002 Peace Process</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sri_Lankan_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 2002 Peace Process">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beginning_of_the_cease-fire">Beginning of the cease-fire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sri_Lankan_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Beginning of the cease-fire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Towards the end of 2001, however, following the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a>, the LTTE began to declare their willingness to explore measures for a peaceful settlement to the conflict. One reason for this action may have been the fear of international pressure and the direct US support of the Sri Lankan government as part of the "war on Terror".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbct_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbct-82">[82]</a></sup> On the other hand, the <a href="/wiki/Covert_operation" title="Covert operation">covert operations</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Long_Range_Reconnaissance_Patrol_(Sri_Lanka)" title="Long Range Reconnaissance Patrol (Sri Lanka)">Long Range Reconnaissance Patrol</a> (LRRP) of Sri Lanka army had a profound impact on the Tiger command structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> During this period, <a href="/wiki/Vaithilingam_Sornalingam" title="Vaithilingam Sornalingam">Vaithilingam Sornalingam</a> <i>alias</i> Shankar, who had been considered the right-hand man of LTTE leader Prabhakaran, and several other high-profile leaders were hunted down and killed by LRRP units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the south the government was facing increasing criticism over its "war for peace" strategy, with peace nowhere in sight and the economy in tatters. After losing a no-confidence motion, President Kumaratunga was forced to dissolve parliament and call for fresh elections. The <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lankan_parliamentary_election,_2001" title="Sri Lankan parliamentary election, 2001">elections, held on 5 December 2001</a>, saw a sweeping victory for the <a href="/wiki/United_National_Front_(Sri_Lanka)" title="United National Front (Sri Lanka)">United National Front</a>, led by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ranil_Wickremasinghe" title="Ranil Wickremasinghe">Ranil Wickremasinghe</a>, who campaigned on a pro-peace platform and pledged to find a negotiated settlement to the conflict.</p>
<p>On 19 December, amidst efforts by Norway to bring the government and the Tamil Tigers to the negotiating table, the LTTE announced a 30-day cease-fire with the Sri Lankan government and pledged to halt all attacks against government forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc2_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc2-85">[85]</a></sup> The new government welcomed the move, and reciprocated it two days later, announcing a month-long cease-fire and agreeing to lift a long-standing economic <a href="/wiki/Embargo" title="Embargo">embargo</a> on rebel-held territory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbct_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbct-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>The cease-fire was by no means acceptable to everyone. Buddhist monks started burning Norwegian flags and agitated against the cease-fire and eventually went to form a political party, <a href="/wiki/Jathika_Hela_Urumaya" title="Jathika Hela Urumaya">Jathika Hela Urumaya</a>, with extremist views.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Signing_of_Memorandum_of_Understanding">Signing of Memorandum of Understanding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sri_Lankan_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Signing of Memorandum of Understanding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<th>3</th>
<td>2–5 December 2002</td>
<td>Radisson SAS Plaza Hotel, <a href="/wiki/Oslo" title="Oslo">Oslo</a>, Norway</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>4</th>
<td>6–9 January 2003</td>
<td>Rose Garden hotel, <a href="/wiki/Nakhon_Pathom" title="Nakhon Pathom">Nakhon Pathom</a>, Thailand</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>5</th>
<td>7–8 February 2003</td>
<td>Norwegian Embassy, Berlin, Germany</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>6</th>
<td>18–21 March 2003</td>
<td>Hakorn Prince Hotel, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kanagawa" title="Kanagawa">Kanagawa</a>, Japan</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The two sides formalized a Memorandum of Understanding (MoU) on 22 February 2002, and signed a permanent cease-fire agreement (CFA). Norway was named <a href="/wiki/Mediation" title="Mediation">mediator</a>, and it was decided that they, together with the other Nordic countries, monitor the cease-fire through a committee of experts named the <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka_Monitoring_Mission" title="Sri Lanka Monitoring Mission">Sri Lanka Monitoring Mission</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc3_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc3-86">[86]</a></sup> In August the government agreed to lift the ban on the LTTE and paved the way for the resumption of direct negotiations with them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc4_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc4-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sea_Tiger_Fast_Attack_boat.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="608" data-file-width="1280" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Sea_Tiger_Fast_Attack_boat.jpg/220px-Sea_Tiger_Fast_Attack_boat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Sea_Tiger_Fast_Attack_boat.jpg/330px-Sea_Tiger_Fast_Attack_boat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Sea_Tiger_Fast_Attack_boat.jpg/440px-Sea_Tiger_Fast_Attack_boat.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sea_Tiger_Fast_Attack_boat.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
LTTE Sea Tiger boat patrolling during the peace</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Following the signing of the ceasefire agreement, commercial air flights to Jaffna began and the LTTE opened the key A9 highway, which linked the government-controlled area in the south with Jaffna and ran through LTTE territory, allowing civilian traffic through the Vanni region for the first time in many years (but only after paying a tax to the LTTE). Many foreign countries also offered substantial financial support if peace was achieved and optimism grew that an end to the decades-long conflict was in sight.</p>
<p>The much-anticipated peace talks began in <a href="/wiki/Phuket_Province" title="Phuket Province">Phuket</a>, Thailand, and further rounds followed in Thailand, Norway, Germany and Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc5_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc5-88">[88]</a></sup> During the talks both sides agreed to the principle of a federal solution and the Tigers dropped their long-standing demand for a separate state. This was a major compromise on the part of the LTTE, which had always insisted on an independent Tamil state. This also represented a compromise on the part of the government, which had seldom agreed to more than minimal devolution. Both sides also exchanged prisoners of war for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_changes_in_the_South">Political changes in the South</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sri_Lankan_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Political changes in the South">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Following the elections of 2001, for the first time in Sri Lanka's history the President and Prime Minister were of two different parties. This co-habitation was uneasy, especially since Prime Minister Wickremasinghe and the UNP favored a federal solution to the conflict, while hard-line elements within President Kumaratunga's party and other Sinhala nationalist groups allied to her opposed one, as they did not trust the LTTE, which continued to levy taxes, strengthen itself by smuggling in arms and ammunition, recruit child soldiers and engage in killings of members of rival Tamil groups and government intelligence agents following the <a href="/wiki/Millennium_City_incident" title="Millennium City incident">Millennium City incident</a>. During this time the LTTE also succeeded in setting up a series of vital bases around the <a href="/wiki/Trincomalee_Harbour" title="Trincomalee Harbour">Trincomalee Harbour</a> (<i>i.e.</i>, Manirasakulam camp) and the Eastern Province.</p>
<p>The talks broke down on 21 April 2003 when the Tamil Tigers announced they were suspending any further talks due to their "displeasure" at the handling of some "critical issues". Among the reasons the Tigers gave were their exclusion from reconstruction talks in Washington, DC, on 14 April and a more general insinuation that they were not receiving the full economic rewards of peace. They cited the failure, as they saw it, of peace dividends to transfer to security withdrawals on the ground and the disparity, as they saw it, between the relative calm of the government-held northeast and continuing violence in Tiger-held areas. However, the LTTE maintained it was committed to a settlement to the two-decade conflict, but stated that progress had to be made on the ground before the settlement proceeded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc6_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc6-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 31 October the LTTE issued its own peace proposal, calling for an <i><a href="/wiki/Interim_Self_Governing_Authority" title="Interim Self Governing Authority">Interim Self Governing Authority</a></i> (ISGA). The ISGA would be fully controlled by the LTTE and would have broad powers in the north and rast (see the <a class="external text" href="http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/world/south_asia/3232913.stm" rel="nofollow">full text of the proposals</a>) This provoked a strong backlash among the hard-line elements in the south, who accused Prime Minister Wickremasinghe of handing the north and east to the LTTE. Under pressure from within her own party to take action, Kumaratunga declared a state of emergency and took three key government ministries, the Ministry of Mass Media, the Interior Ministry and the crucial Defense Ministry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc7_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc7-91">[91]</a></sup> She then formed an alliance with the JVP, called the <a href="/wiki/United_People%27s_Freedom_Alliance" title="United People's Freedom Alliance">United People's Freedom Alliance</a>, opposed to the ISGA and advocating a harder line on the LTTE, and called for fresh elections. The <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lankan_parliamentary_election,_2004" title="Sri Lankan parliamentary election, 2004">elections</a>, held on 8 April 2004, resulted in victory for the <a href="/wiki/United_People%27s_Freedom_Alliance" title="United People's Freedom Alliance">UPFA</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahinda_Rajapakse" title="Mahinda Rajapakse">Mahinda Rajapakse</a> appointed as Prime Minister.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Split_of_the_LTTE">Split of the LTTE</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sri_Lankan_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Split of the LTTE">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Meanwhile, in March 2004 there had been a major split between the northern and eastern wings of the LTTE. <a href="/wiki/Vinayagamoorthy_Muralitharan" title="Vinayagamoorthy Muralitharan">Vinayagamoorthy Muralitharan</a> <i>alias</i> Col. Karuna, the Eastern commander of the LTTE and one of Prabhakaran's trusted lieutenants, pulled 5,000 eastern cadres out of the LTTE, claiming insufficient resources and power were being given to Tamils of the eastern part of the island. It was the biggest expression of dissension in the history of the LTTE and a clash within the LTTE seemed imminent. After the parliamentary elections, brief fighting south of Trincomalee led to a rapid retreat and capitulation of Karuna's group, their leaders eventually going into hiding including Karuna himself, who was helped to escape by <a href="/wiki/Seyed_Ali_Zahir_Moulana" title="Seyed Ali Zahir Moulana">Seyed Ali Zahir Moulana</a>, a politician from the ruling party. However, the "Karuna faction" maintained a significant presence in the east and continued to launch attacks against the LTTE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt2_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt2-92">[92]</a></sup> The LTTE accused the army of covertly backing the breakaway group, which subsequently formed a political party named the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TamilEela_Makkal_Viduthalai_Pulikal" title="TamilEela Makkal Viduthalai Pulikal">TamilEela Makkal Viduthalai Pulikal</a> (TMVP) and hopef to contest in future elections.</p>
<p>The cease-fire largely held through all this turmoil, with over 3000 infractions by the LTTE and some 300 by the SLA recorded by the Sri Lanka Monitoring Mission (SLMM) by 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> The situation was further complicated by allegations that both sides were carrying out covert operations against each other. The government claimed that the LTTE was killing political opponents, recruiting children, importing arms and killing government security and intelligence officers. The rebels accused the government of supporting paramilitary groups against them, especially the Karuna group.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tsunami_and_aftermath">Tsunami and aftermath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sri_Lankan_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Tsunami and aftermath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On 26 December 2004, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean_tsunami" title="Indian Ocean tsunami">Indian Ocean tsunami</a> hit Sri Lanka, killing more than 35,000 people and leaving many more homeless. Aid poured in from donor countries, but disagreements arose instantly over how it should be distributed to the Tamil regions under LTTE control. By 24 June the government and LTTE agreed on the Post-Tsunami Operational Management Structure (P-TOMS), but it received sharp criticism from the <a href="/wiki/Janatha_Vimukthi_Peramuna" title="Janatha Vimukthi Peramuna">JVP</a>, who left the government in protest. The legality of P-TOMS was also challenged in the courts. President Kumaratunga eventually had to scrap P-TOMS, which led to widespread criticism that sufficient aid was not reaching the north and east of the country. However, immediately following the tsunami there was a marked decrease in violence in the north.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Sri Lankan Foreign Minister <a href="/wiki/Lakshman_Kadirgamar" title="Lakshman Kadirgamar">Lakshman Kadirgamar</a>, a Tamil who was highly respected by foreign diplomats and who had been sharply critical of the LTTE, was assassinated at his home on 12 August 2005, allegedly by an LTTE sniper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> His assassination led to the marginalization of the LTTE from the international community, and is generally considered to be the moment when the LTTE lost much of its sympathy in the eyes of foreign nations. Hence the silence of the international community when the Sri Lankan government took military action against the LTTE in 2006, when the latter closed the Mavil aru sluice.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag needs further explanation. (July 2012)">further explanation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Further political change occurred when the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_Sri_Lanka" title="Supreme Court of Sri Lanka">Supreme Court of Sri Lanka</a> declared President Kumaratunga's second and final term over and ordered her to hold fresh presidential elections.
